Student Name: Arnold Atchoe.¶

Repository link: https://github.com/kwameape123/620-mod6-web-scraping¶

Perform the tasks described in the Markdown cells below. When you have completed the assignment make sure your code cells have all been run (and have output beneath them) and ensure you have committed and pushed ALL of your changes to your assignment repository.

Every question that requires you to write code will have a code cell underneath it; you may either write your entire solution in that cell or write it in a python file (.py), then import and run the appropriate code to answer the question.

STEP 3(QUESTION 1): SAVE WEB PAGE ARTICLE AS A FILE.¶

Write code that extracts the article html from https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/ and dumps it to a .pkl (or other appropriate file)

In [8]:
# Use requests to fetch the web page
url = 'https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/'
article = requests.get(url)

# View the status code to ensure the request was successful
if article.status_code == 200:
    print("web page fetched successfully.")
else:print(f"fetch error, status code: {article.status_code}")

# View the content type to ensure it's HTML
print("Content type:", article.headers['Content-Type'])

# View the html content
article_content = article.text
print(article_content)

# save the article html to a pickle file
with open('article.pkl', 'wb') as file:
    pickle.dump(article_content, file)

# or save the article html to a text file
with open('article.txt', 'w', encoding='utf-8') as file:
    file.write(article_content)

# Note that beautifulsoup4 can read either the pickle file or the text file

<article itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Article" id="post-466450" class="post-466450 post type-post status-publish format-standard has-post-thumbnail hentry category-car-hacks category-engineering category-featured category-laser-hacks category-slider tag-laser tag-laser-headlight tag-laser-headlights tag-light">
    <header class="entry-header">
        <h1 class="entry-title" itemprop="name">How Laser Headlights Work</h1>
        <div class="entry-meta">
            <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comments" class="comments-counts"><span class="icon-hackaday icon-hackaday-comment"></span>
                130 Comments            </a>
            <ul class="meta-authors vcard author">
                <li>by:</li>
                <span class="fn"><a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/author/lewinday/" title="Posts by Lewin Day" class="author url fn" rel="author">Lewin Day</a></span>
            </ul>
        </div><!-- .entry-meta -->

        <div class="entry-meta entry-meta-last">
            <span class="entry-date published updated" itemprop="datePublished" content="March 22, 2021"><a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/" title="March 22, 2021 - 7:01 am">March 22, 2021</a></span>
            <ul class="share-post">
                <li><a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://www.facebook.com/sharer/sharer.php?u=https%3A%2F%2Fhackaday.com%2F2021%2F03%2F22%2Fhow-laser-headlights-work%2F" target="_blank"><span class="icon-hackaday icon-hackaday-facebook"></span></a></li>
                <li><a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://twitter.com/intent/tweet?text=How+Laser+Headlights+Work via @hackaday&amp;url=https%3A%2F%2Fhackaday.com%2F2021%2F03%2F22%2Fhow-laser-headlights-work%2F" target="_blank"><span class="icon-hackaday icon-hackaday-twitter"></span></a></li>
            </ul>
        </div><!-- .entry-meta -->

        <div class="entry-featured-image"><img itemprop="image" content="https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/LaserHeadlight-thumb.jpg?w=600&amp;h=600" src="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/LaserHeadlight.jpg?w=800" alt=""></div>
    </header><!-- .entry-header -->

    <div class="entry-content" itemprop="articleBody">
        <p>When we think about the onward march of automotive technology, headlights aren&#8217;t usually the first thing that come to mind. Engines, fuel efficiency, and the switch to electric power are all more front of mind. However, that doesn&#8217;t mean there aren&#8217;t thousands of engineers around the world working to improve the state of the art in automotive lighting day in, day out.</p>
<p>Sealed beam headlights gave way to more modern designs once regulations loosened up, while bulbs moved from simple halogens to xenon HIDs and, more recently, LEDs. Now, a new technology is on the scene, with lasers!</p>
<p><span id="more-466450"></span></p>
<h2>Laser Headlights?!</h2>
<figure id="attachment_466463" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-466463" style="width: 290px" class="wp-caption alignright"><img data-attachment-id="466463" data-permalink="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/laser-light-bwm-proto-2/" data-orig-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-light-bwm-proto-2.jpg" data-orig-size="290,193" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="laser light bwm proto 2" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-light-bwm-proto-2.jpg?w=290" data-large-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-light-bwm-proto-2.jpg?w=290" loading="lazy" class="size-medium wp-image-466463" src="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-light-bwm-proto-2.jpg?w=290" alt="" width="290" height="193" srcset="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-light-bwm-proto-2.jpg 290w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-light-bwm-proto-2.jpg?resize=250,166 250w" sizes="(max-width: 290px) 100vw, 290px"/><figcaption id="caption-attachment-466463" class="wp-caption-text">BWM&#8217;s prototype laser headlight assemblies undergoing testing.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The first image brought to mind by the phrase &#8220;laser headlights&#8221; is that of laser beams firing out the front of an automobile. Obviously, coherent beams of monochromatic light would make for poor illumination outside of a very specific spot quite some distance away. Thankfully for our eyes, laser headlights don&#8217;t work in this way at all.</p>
<p>Instead, laser headlights consist of one or more solid state laser diodes mounted inside the headlight. These blue lasers are fired at a yellow phosphor, similar to that used in white LEDs. This produces a powerful, vibrant white light that can then be bounced off reflectors and out of the headlights towards the road. Laser headlights built in this way have several benefits. They&#8217;re more energy efficient than LEDs that put out the same amount of light, while also being more space efficient, too.</p>
<figure id="attachment_466464" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-466464" style="width: 400px" class="wp-caption alignleft"><img data-attachment-id="466464" data-permalink="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/laser-headlight-bmw-i8/" data-orig-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg" data-orig-size="1900,1265" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="laser headlight bmw i8" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?w=400" data-large-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?w=800" loading="lazy" class="size-medium wp-image-466464" src="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?w=400" alt="" width="400" height="266" srcset="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg 1900w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?resize=250,166 250w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?resize=400,266 400w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?resize=800,533 800w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlight-bmw-i8.jpg?resize=1536,1023 1536w" sizes="(max-width: 400px) 100vw, 400px"/><figcaption id="caption-attachment-466464" class="wp-caption-text">BWM&#8217;s futuristic i8 was one of the first vehicles to ship with laser headlight technology.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Laser headlights are still a nascent technology, thus far only appearing in a few BMW, Audi, and other select vehicles. <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://www.osram.com/am/specials/trends-in-automotive-lighting/laser-light-new-headlight-technology/questions-and-answers-on-innovative-laser-technology/index.jsp" target="_blank">BMW&#8217;s technology was developed in partnership with lighting experts OSRAM</a>. In practice, a regular LED low-beam lamp is used, with the laser used to create an incredibly bright and focused spot, used for high-beams. This can provide illumination out to 600 meters ahead of the vehicle, double that of conventional LED high beams. The lights use indium gallium nitride diode lasers that were originally used in projectors, with power levels above 1 watt. One of the challenges in implementing such technology in an automotive environment is the need for it to operate at temperature extremes. While research lasers and laser pointers may primarily be used at typical room temperatures, automotive headlights must be able to withstand everything from 40 degrees below zero up to 50 degrees C. Thankfully, the laser&#8217;s high efficiency means it doesn&#8217;t have huge heat output of its own to further complicate things. Other engineering challenges involve tailoring the optics package for the rough-and-tumble, high vibration environment found in the automotive application. It&#8217;s also important to ensure, as with any such device, that the end user can&#8217;t be exposed to harmful laser radiation in the event of accident or malfunction.</p>
<h2>Tearing Down the Laser Headlight</h2>
<figure id="attachment_466457" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-466457" style="width: 400px" class="wp-caption alignright"><img data-attachment-id="466457" data-permalink="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/ledheadlights-aliexp/" data-orig-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg" data-orig-size="930,930" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="ledheadlights-aliexp" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg?w=400" data-large-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg?w=625" loading="lazy" class="size-medium wp-image-466457" src="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg?w=400" alt="" width="400" height="400" srcset="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg 930w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg?resize=250,250 250w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg?resize=400,400 400w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/ledheadlights-aliexp.jpg?resize=625,625 625w" sizes="(max-width: 400px) 100vw, 400px"/><figcaption id="caption-attachment-466457" class="wp-caption-text">A marketing image showing the construction of an aftermarket LED/laser headlight. We&#8217;d take the laser power with a grain of salt &#8212; it&#8217;s difficult to imagine a 10 W laser shining directly on some small LEDs without melting a hole through the board in short order.</figcaption></figure>
<p>An aftermarket has sprung up too, with delightfully innovative designs. Combined laser/LED headlights are readily available on Alibaba, <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://www.aliexpress.com/item/1005001889968976.html?spm=a2g0o.search0302.0.0.5bebc1d45ruE7k&amp;algo_pvid=b016ca5c-d0fe-4309-ab5f-e39ea8770c80&amp;algo_expid=b016ca5c-d0fe-4309-ab5f-e39ea8770c80-0&amp;btsid=0b0a556516155071180373533e897a&amp;ws_ab_test=searchweb0_0,searchweb201602_,searchweb201603_" target="_blank">designed as a drop in replacement for projector lamps on existing vehicles.</a> These often use an LED low-beam, and a combined LED/laser high beam, where the laser diode shoots directly at the LED phosphor to excite it further, rather than using its own. These units often also come with fan cooling to keep the laser and LEDs below their maximum operational temperature. Such developments are exciting, though it&#8217;s important to be wary of the performance of unknown aftermarket headlights. <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://jalopnik.com/why-most-led-headlight-upgrades-dont-really-work-an-ex-1843070472" target="_blank">Many aftermarket LED headlight &#8220;upgrades&#8221; fail to pass muster</a> when it comes to real-world performance, and there&#8217;s no reason to believe hybrid LED/laser designs will be any different. We&#8217;d love to pass a selection of these parts through <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/http://www.vdrsyd.com/ancap_datasheets/xprotocol_archive/IIHS/headlight_test_rating_protocol.pdf" target="_blank">a full IIHS test protocol</a>, but that&#8217;s sadly beyond the scope (and budget!) of this article.</p>
<p>However, [mikeselectricstuff] has happened to lay his hands on both the BMW and aftermarket parts, <a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7fRjMHtnShs" target="_blank">tearing them all down in his workshop to see what makes them tick.</a> The differences are multitude when laid bare on the bench. The AliExpress part is relatively simple, wired up no differently from a regular headlight. Interestingly, however, the laser high-beam circuit runs all the time in these parts. To prevent blinding other road users, a shutter is kept in place to block the light, which is moved out of the way with a solenoid when the driver turns on the high beam switch.</p>
<p>Where the aftermarket part is a little out of left field, the BMW design is another thing entirely. The cutting-edge headlights are hooked up with multiple connectors and over 30 conductors, with much of the driver electronics living in an external controller. Much of this is to drive the various LEDs and stepper motors for slewing the headlights when steering. However, the laser assembly brings its own complexities. Twin light sensors are built inside to monitor the laser beam, and a special metal blocking arm sits directly in front of the diode, presumably to stop the laser light leaving the headlight in the event the phosphor coating burns through. It&#8217;s truly wild to get a look inside a modern luxury car&#8217;s headlight and see just how far we&#8217;ve come from the old days of simple sealed beams.</p>
<h2>Cost Versus Performance</h2>
<p>Despite the efficiency gains available, the technology remains expensive. Powerful laser diodes don&#8217;t come cheap, after all. However, as the technology trickles down to lower-end models, it&#8217;s likely that we could see economies of scale change that for the better. Indeed, if national authorities begin to demand higher performance headlights as standard, we could see laser headlights become the norm, rather than an expensive luxury. The technology could naturally be applied to home and commercial lighting, too &#8212; though we suspect the potential gains are limited enough that LED lighting will remain the norm for some time to come.</p>
<figure id="attachment_466459" aria-describedby="caption-attachment-466459" style="width: 400px" class="wp-caption alignright"><img data-attachment-id="466459" data-permalink="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/laser-headlights-bmw-laser/" data-orig-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlights-bmw-laser.jpg" data-orig-size="796,316" data-comments-opened="1" data-image-meta="{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}" data-image-title="laser headlights bmw laser" data-image-description="" data-medium-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlights-bmw-laser.jpg?w=400" data-large-file="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlights-bmw-laser.jpg?w=796" loading="lazy" class="size-medium wp-image-466459" src="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlights-bmw-laser.jpg?w=400" alt="" width="400" height="159" srcset="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlights-bmw-laser.jpg 796w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlights-bmw-laser.jpg?resize=250,99 250w, https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005im_/https://hackaday.com/wp-content/uploads/2021/03/laser-headlights-bmw-laser.jpg?resize=400,159 400w" sizes="(max-width: 400px) 100vw, 400px"/><figcaption id="caption-attachment-466459" class="wp-caption-text">The high light output of laser headlights in a compact package allows engineers greater freedom when designing the front-end of a car.</figcaption></figure>
<p>As it currently stands, much of the appeal of the new technology is about the packaging benefits, which allow automotive designers greater freedom around the headlight area. Such concerns are less of a factor when it comes to light fittings in the home or office, or indeed, on lower-end automobiles. Regardless, it&#8217;s an exciting new application for lasers and one we&#8217;ll be sure to see more of in the future.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>As someone in my 50s, with naturally deteriorating eyesight, I am frequently dazzled by insanely bright HID or LED headlights.</p>
<p>Laser excited phosphor headlights just sound like an tech overcomplication for up market German performance cars. Most people don&#8217;t want to, or are unlikely to ever drive at 240km/h at night on a German autobahn.</p>
<p>Light emissions from these vehicles can be just as offensive as their tail-pipe emissions.</p>
<p>If the designers are bored and want to put more tech into vehicles just for the sake of it, they might consider an infrared floodlight and an IR camera connected to a head-up-display. </p>
<p>If you need to see 600m ahead of you in the dark &#8211; you are probably driving too fast.</p>

										<b class="fn">Dude</b> <span class="says">says:</span>				</div><!-- .comment-author -->

				<div class="comment-metadata">
					<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333353">
						<time datetime="2021-03-22T10:04:51-07:00">
							March 22, 2021 at 10:04 am						</time>
					</a>
									</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->

							</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>I don&#8217;t think so. An average car can do a hard stop within 70 meters from 70 kph (19.5 m/s) and that&#8217;s giving you two seconds of reaction time. Assuming linear deceleration, your average stopping time will be between 7-8 seconds. If you take twice that time, your brakes are shot.</p>
<p>Second point is, assuming average eyesight, the smallest distinct object you can just about make out from 600 meters away would be the size of a football, if it has enough contrast with the background, but that would require daylight levels of illumination, and no vibration and shaking from the vehicle. In the dark, no matter how bright your headlights are, you would be lucky to make out an object the size of a truck at that distance. It&#8217;s just gonna be a blur.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>&#8220;Assuming linear deceleration, your average stopping time will be between 7-8 seconds. If you take twice that time, your brakes are shot.&#8221;</p>
<p>Unless the road&#8217;s wet, in which case the general estimate is a factor of two increase in stopping distance, which hey, gets you to around that 15 second mark (okay, maybe 12-13 seconds, I did put an &#8216;approximate&#8217; there). You don&#8217;t design safety for the *best* conditions. Should&#8217;ve clarified that better, though.</p>
<p>&#8220;Second point is, assuming average eyesight, the smallest distinct object you can just about make out from 600 meters away would be the size of a football&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8230; I&#8217;m sorry, do you generally worry about hitting footballs on the road? I&#8217;m pretty sure the main things I worry about on roads (deer, vehicles, trees) are *way* bigger than footballs.</p>
<p>Besides, the main point isn&#8217;t seeing things at 600 m. It&#8217;s seeing things farther than ~200 m away, which is where normal high beams cut out.</p>

<p>vis-a-vie, when you flick a light switch off, you can&#8217;t see shit, but if you sit in that dark room for 20 minutes you&#8217;ll be able to see everything provided you don&#8217;t have poor eyesight. </p>

<p>there should be a small tax on the sale of every vehicle that goes toward adding a diffused ambient light to all major roadways so that way headlights are not even required.   </p>
<p>and yes, diffused so that way the lights are not contributing to light pollution, and yes, we do have the technology (and it&#8217;s cheap) to create lighting in such a way. </p>

They have had multiple iterations trying to do the same thing.  this comes from someone who worked at their manufacturing facilities. those headlights are a giant pile of irritating garbage and you will get my brights turned on every time i see them, because they don&#8217;t adjust properly or fast enough. it&#8217;s always too late, the tech literally doesn&#8217;t work well enough and they&#8217;re blowing a ton of money on learning how to properly blind the crap out of everyone on the road&#8230; but you can&#8217;t tell a german that they are wrong and their attempt to over engineer their way out of a problem is dumb&#8230; believe me, it&#8217;s a lost cause.</p>

				<p>There is a demo of a system like this that used a DPL projector and camera that took this idea to the extreme.  It was able to steer the headlights around rain and snow in real-time.  Made a big difference too!</p>

The DOT  has lagged behind for decades, not because they&#8217;re dullards but from states having political bearing on lighting standards for vehicles. Finally, when round sealed beams went away as hood lines were lowered giving manufacturers more room to design custom headlights, we&#8217;re now flooded with halogen, hid, led and laser headlights. Politics will always interfere with science, engineering and technology.</p>

	<li id="comment-6333330" class="comment even depth-4 parent ">
		<article id="div-comment-6333330" class="comment-body">
			<footer class="comment-meta">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
										<b class="fn"><a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/http://hypospeak.wordpress.com/" rel="external nofollow ugc" class="url">jack324</a></b> <span class="says">says:</span>				</div><!-- .comment-author -->

				<div class="comment-metadata">
					<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333330">
						<time datetime="2021-03-22T08:52:31-07:00">
							March 22, 2021 at 8:52 am						</time>
					</a>
									</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->

							</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>The arm is fixed, and is almost certainly hefty enough to absorb 1-10w if laser energy without heating up all that much, so no burn through. Also, there&#8217;s no misalignment if the the laser housing is placed in a premachined or cast part with no option for adjusting the alignment. So basically as is designed, if the phosphor coating is destroyed and the arm isn&#8217;t a mirror, then all that happens is the lasert light gets scattered around the headlight interior and at best some leaks out but due to light spreading out, unless you where right up on the spot where the phosphor was you wouldn&#8217;t get enough laser energy hitting your eye to do any damage.</p>
<p>Stop worrying about imagined danger when there isn&#8217;t any.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->
			<div class="report-abuse">
                
			<span id="safe-comments-result-6333330"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm('Are you sure you want to report this comment?')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( '6333330', '84066847aa', 'safe-comments-result-6333330'):'';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>

			<footer class="comment-meta">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
										<b class="fn">Foldi-One</b> <span class="says">says:</span>				</div><!-- .comment-author -->

				<div class="comment-metadata">
					<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333346">

							March 22, 2021 at 9:40 am						</time>
					</a>
									</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->

							</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>With the vibrations, poor maintenance and how little laser energy it needs to damage the eye I&#8217;d say there is a very real danger still. If you can be 100% certain the heatsinking and orientation of the this blocking arm you are not putting it in a car in the real world, where the sudden shock of hitting a pot hole or curb and the generally harsh environment with constant vibrations of a bad road has broken things its not supposed to be able to many many times now&#8230;</p>
<p>I&#8217;m sure its perfectly safe as a technology in the lab, will be fine in cars for a while, but at some point the housing will crack/bend, the arm will corrode, water can ingress etc etc, and being an invisible problem there will be stray lasers that can cause serious eye damage out there, because nobody noticed the failure yet (when its throwing dazzingly bright light hundreds of meters by design even shedding a sizeable percentage won&#8217;t be obvious to the driver much of the time, and cracked parts or water inside are not always visible without complete disassembly and possibly x-rays either)</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>Those indicators are too distracting even during the day, and worse at night. If I&#8217;m ever forced to own a car with them then my first &#8216;customization&#8217; will involve diagonal cutters. Failing that, scuffing the lenses with sandpaper, followed by a coating of steel-filled epoxy.</p>
<p>Effective and safe automotive illumination was a solved problem 30 years ago, and most subsequent &#8216;improvements&#8217; represent a triumph of style over substance. Even a lot of LED lighting is a distracting and annoying nuisance, except in those instances where the PWM frequency is high enough where my eyes see only a faint flicker instead of the illusion that the lights are jumping around in mid-air.</p>

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>This does really worry me. I already have problems with many HID headlights that are from such a focused beam of light that they are piercing at night and nearly blind me with oncoming traffic. This is only going to make it worse.</p>
<p>We need much more diffuse light, not more concentrated light. Use LEDs across the entire front of the car &#8211; that will give much more light but also more diffuse so that it&#8217;s not going to kill my night vision every time I drive by one.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->

<p>&gt;&#8221;We’d take the laser power with a grain of salt — it’s difficult to imagine a 10 W laser shining directly on some small LEDs without melting a hole through the board in short order.&#8221; </p>

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>Why should one use UV when intense blue light is available? UV has higher quantum energy than blue, which has to be dropped by the fluorescent phosphor to different VIS wavelengths. So blue can be more efficient than UV  at the stage of the phosphor. Not to mention: blue LEDs are very efficient, UV LEDS are not.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>I am very pleased that this is a real problem and not only in my mind. I have been cussing the led lights the past  10 years with the last 4 years really being a living hell driving in the dark during rush hours. My solution was to tone those car windows that the law permits over here and additonally gluing plastic toning films on the side windows so the films occultate the side mirrors when driving. An imperfect solution but in a pinch i can slide down the side windows and have normal mirror brightness when reversing in those dark winter months. The led low beams are a bigger issue for me since I am constantly exposed to vans, big ass volvo, audi and bmw cars that just have to drive right up to my tailpipe before overtaking.</p>

<p>Many low power auto bulbs already have LED equivalents, and there are some H4 and H7 plug in headlamp LED units &#8211; but most require additional heatsinks.</p>

And it would appear that the complainants are about to own the defendant&#8217;s truck, mostly because the defendant&#8217;s insurance company abandoned him due to non-compliant vehicle accessories. But short of legal heroics via the civil courts, we need to encourage our respective national automotive engineering and law enforcement agencies to act on this increasing scourge of emissions that are nothing short of an assault via photons.</p>

	<li id="comment-6333806" class="comment even depth-2  ">
		<article id="div-comment-6333806" class="comment-body">
			<footer class="comment-meta">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
										<b class="fn">three_d_dave</b> <span class="says">says:</span>				</div><!-- .comment-author -->

				<div class="comment-metadata">
					<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333806">
						<time datetime="2021-03-23T15:00:02-07:00">
							March 23, 2021 at 3:00 pm						</time>
					</a>
									</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->

							</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>Today they are for high beams. Then it will be for &#8220;safety&#8221; that it is transitioned to low beams. </p>
<p>Recall that the SAE standards for low-beams assumed that the maximum illumination would be in the central area aligned with the filament, with fall-off to the edges to avoid a sharp transition, and then lamp makers used optics to drive the maximum allowable illumination to 100% of the field with no gradual drop-off, creating that blinding flash transition so popular today. Of course they also did that test with the mandated 12V supply even though autos run theirs at 14-15V, for a gain of around 20-40% in brightness over the test condition. At least with LEDs that lie no longer works, but the elimination of gradual falloff more than makes up. And, the original lamps had a spectrum with little blue in it; now that component that destroys night vision, is a large component.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->
			<div class="report-abuse">
                
			<span id="safe-comments-result-6333806"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm('Are you sure you want to report this comment?')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( '6333806', '84066847aa', 'safe-comments-result-6333806'):'';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>

	<li id="comment-6333743" class="comment even depth-4  ">
		<article id="div-comment-6333743" class="comment-body">
			<footer class="comment-meta">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
										<b class="fn">Dude</b> <span class="says">says:</span>				</div><!-- .comment-author -->

				<div class="comment-metadata">
					<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333743">
						<time datetime="2021-03-23T11:16:51-07:00">
							March 23, 2021 at 11:16 am						</time>
					</a>
									</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->

							</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>Seriously though. Air resistance starts to dominate over rolling resistance around 20 mph and up.</p>
<p>In stop/go traffic, you&#8217;re never going very fast. Each acceleration to 25 mph in a two-tonne car takes about 120 kJ of energy, which is worth 6 seconds of driving on the highway at 20 kW (27 HP) average power. Most of the fuel consumption is due to idle loss, which consumes about 1-2 kW to keep the belts turning while you&#8217;re waiting at the red lights.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->

										<b class="fn">Lewin Day</b> <span class="says">says:</span>				</div><!-- .comment-author -->

				<div class="comment-metadata">
					<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333859">
						<time datetime="2021-03-23T19:19:54-07:00">
							March 23, 2021 at 7:19 pm						</time>
					</a>
									</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->

							</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>Greater efficiency means less power turned into heat. This means you can have brighter light in a more compact package without worrying about everything melting.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>70kph is 43.5 mph. The average response time to braking is around 0.75 sec. All braking stats are related to stock tires on dry surfaces. At 45 mph, an emergency stop wouldn&#8217;t exceed 100 feet/30 meters (dry surface).</p>
<p>Anyone needing 600 meters of vehicle lighting is either driving at very high speed (Autobahn?) or reckless on USA roads. 600 meters is 1968.5 feet,  well over 1/4 mile. My guess is anyone speeding may need high beams on isolated roads otherwise the majority of USA roads are limited between 55-65 mph. Low beams are effective to 200 feet/61 meters.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>You had this same misconception last time we talked about light efficiency, and you&#8217;re still wrong. That 44% figure is <b>efficacy</b>, not efficiency. That means it is <i>relative to an single-color 550nm (yellow-green) light source</i> and you wouldn&#8217;t want to light a room with that anyway.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->
			<div class="report-abuse">
                
			<span id="safe-comments-result-6334871"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm('Are you sure you want to report this comment?')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( '6334871', '84066847aa', 'safe-comments-result-6334871'):'';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>

	<li id="comment-6334949" class="comment even depth-4 parent ">
		<article id="div-comment-6334949" class="comment-body">
			<footer class="comment-meta">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
										<b class="fn">Dude</b> <span class="says">says:</span>				</div><!-- .comment-author -->

				<div class="comment-metadata">
					<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6334949">
						<time datetime="2021-03-27T05:52:43-07:00">
							March 27, 2021 at 5:52 am						</time>
					</a>

							</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>No. You got it exactly wrong the last time. Efficacy refers to the number of lumens per Watt of the radiation, and luminous efficiency refers to the perceived brightness relative to 555 nm light. Then there is the overall luminous efficacy and efficiency of the source:</p>
<p><a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luminous_efficacy#Lighting_efficiency" rel="nofollow ugc" target="_blank">https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luminous_efficacy#Lighting_efficiency</a></p>
<p>&#8220;The main difference between the luminous efficacy of radiation and the luminous efficacy of a source is that the latter accounts for input energy that is lost as heat or otherwise&#8221;<br/>
&#8220;The following table lists luminous efficacy of a source and efficiency for various light sources&#8221;</p>
<p>&#8220;Theoretical limit for a white LED with phosphorescence color mixing 260–300 lm/W 38.1–43.9%&#8221; </p>
<p>This IS counting &#8220;watts photons visible light out  ÷ watts electrons in&#8221;.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->
			<div class="report-abuse">
                
			<span id="safe-comments-result-6334949"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm('Are you sure you want to report this comment?')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( '6334949', '84066847aa', 'safe-comments-result-6334949'):'';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>
			<div class="reply"><a rel="nofollow" class="comment-reply-link" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/?replytocom=6334949#respond" data-commentid="6334949" data-postid="466450" data-belowelement="div-comment-6334949" data-respondelement="respond" data-replyto="Reply to Dude" aria-label="Reply to Dude">Reply</a></div>		</article><!-- .comment-body -->
	<ol class="children">
	<li id="comment-6334951" class="comment odd alt depth-5  max-depth">
		<article id="div-comment-6334951" class="comment-body">
			<footer class="comment-meta">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
										<b class="fn">Dude</b> <span class="says">says:</span>				</div><!-- .comment-author -->

				<div class="comment-metadata">
					<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6334951">
						<time datetime="2021-03-27T06:05:30-07:00">
							March 27, 2021 at 6:05 am						</time>
					</a>
									</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->

							</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>Of course here as well, the ideal 555 nm source is by definition 100% efficient. It converts energy perfectly to photons and produces light that is perceived the brightest.</p>
<p>A &#8220;perfect white&#8221; source with no source losses and a color temperature of 5800 K, basically a black body spectrum truncated to the perceivable colors, would have an overall luminous efficiency of 37%. This improves as you make the spectrum narrower. Likewise, LED lights are able to overcome this only by compromising on the light quality with a CRI less than 100.</p>
<p>If you have a white LED with a claimed luminous efficiency of 75%, I have some news for you: either they&#8217;re lying, or it&#8217;s nowhere near white.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->
			<div class="report-abuse">
                
			<span id="safe-comments-result-6334951"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm('Are you sure you want to report this comment?')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( '6334951', '84066847aa', 'safe-comments-result-6334951'):'';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>
					</article><!-- .comment-body -->
	</li><!-- #comment-## -->
	<li id="comment-6334954" class="comment even depth-5  max-depth">
		<article id="div-comment-6334954" class="comment-body">
			<footer class="comment-meta">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
										<b class="fn">Dude</b> <span class="says">says:</span>				</div><!-- .comment-author -->

				<div class="comment-metadata">
					<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6334954">
						<time datetime="2021-03-27T06:17:33-07:00">
							March 27, 2021 at 6:17 am						</time>
					</a>
									</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->

							</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>Or to make it absolutely clear: </p>
<p>An ideal white LED with 100% source efficiency from electricity to radiation is 38.1–43.9% efficient in terms of visible light.</p>
<p>All real world LEDs are much worse than this^, with typical source efficiencies in the 20-30% range, which drops the practical luminous efficiency down from the theoretical max 300 lm/W to around 75-100 lm/W and the overall luminous efficiency becomes 10-15%</p>
<p>^(due to: heat effects, ballast losses, light losses in the LED casing, reflector and diffuser losses&#8230;)</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->
			<div class="report-abuse">
                
			<span id="safe-comments-result-6334954"><a class="hide-if-no-js" href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="return confirm('Are you sure you want to report this comment?')?safe_report_comments_flag_comment( '6334954', '84066847aa', 'safe-comments-result-6334954'):'';">Report comment</a></span>            </div>

<p>For example, the upcoming 2022 EU regulations mandate that all new cars have Automated Emergency Braking which can detect pedestrians and cyclists, Intelligent Speed Assistance which stops you from speeding by lifting the throttle and blaring alarms in your ear if you override it for too long, and a black box that records the last few seconds up to a crash.</p>

				<p>Wonders if adding a selectable filter to stop blue laser light might make it somewhat more useful.<br/>
Also using more than one emitter material ie warm and cool white, but that would be overcomplicated.<br/>
I did come up with a variant using RGB LDs with a PS3 beam combiner and it should work but am lacking<br/>
a spare working &gt;15mW blue PL450 LD the same as the unit here.<br/>
Plan is to have a beam scanner so each side scans half the road with an eye-safe divergent beam,<br/>
doubling as an indicator and other functions.<br/>
User configured brightness depending on ambient light level and selective blanking so it turns off/dims<br/>
when scanning a large approaching object with its own light source(s).</p>

				<div class="comment-metadata">

							</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>A friend of mine who did rally car racing with his subaru once told me that you shouldnt mix the color temperatures of your lights too much, with the exception of yellow fog lights being effective for snow situations.  The convo came up because he was replacing his headlight bulbs to match the color temperature of his new lightbar.  I don&#8217;t remember his explanation of why it was important to try to match color temperatures, but it made sense at the time.  </p>
<p>Being able to change color temperatures on the fly sounds like a great idea though, and relatively easy to implement &#8211; there are definitely situations where warm white is advantageous (bad weather driving, seeing detail of snow covered roads) and other situations where cool white offers more performance (lets your eyes see further assuming clear skies.  Ive also heard the really high color temperatures can reduce eye fatigue).</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>Instead of a laser and its inherent risk, use an electron beam to excite the phosphor. All you need is an electron gun and an electromagnetic focusing ring and some high voltage to attract the electrons and put it in a vacuum and use some lenses to project the light. Very old tech made new and much safer than lasers.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->

				<p>A comment if I may, I am visually impaired, considered legally blind and even<br/>
regular headlights hurt. I don&#8217;t drive but as a passenger, someimes it seems like<br/>
I&#8217;m looking at the sun when headlights hit my eyes. Couple this with a recent<br/>
retina detachment (fixed) and a cataract (yet to be fixed), and these bright<br/>
lights are just torture. I will say that bicycle lighting has improved since<br/>
the days of the little dynamo powered flashlight bulb that was meant for<br/>
drivers to see you instead of you lighting up the road in front of you.</p>
<p><a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://youtu.be/9MAkl8tEsnA" rel="nofollow ugc" target="_blank">https://youtu.be/9MAkl8tEsnA</a></p>
<p>How about low power bulbs coupled with a winshield that amplifies the ambient light<br/>
while blocking direct light?</p>

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>Think about optics: A windshield that works as light amplifier is no solution, it would only blind you with glare. You would need an image intensifier, like a night vision device. And that is not possible as a windshield.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->
			<div class="report-abuse">

	<li id="comment-6333542" class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 parent ">
		<article id="div-comment-6333542" class="comment-body">
			<footer class="comment-meta">
				<div class="comment-author vcard">
										<b class="fn">DC</b> <span class="says">says:</span>				</div><!-- .comment-author -->

				<div class="comment-metadata">
					<a href="https://web.archive.org/web/20210327165005/https://hackaday.com/2021/03/22/how-laser-headlights-work/#comment-6333542">
						<time datetime="2021-03-22T18:31:22-07:00">
							March 22, 2021 at 6:31 pm						</time>
					</a>
									</div><!-- .comment-metadata -->

							</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>I &#8220;upgraded&#8221; my SUVs headlights from halogen bulbs with reflector housing to LED projectors (the mid-generation refresh of my car included projector headlights and LED tail lights, so my &#8220;upgrade&#8221; was OEM.  I absolutely hated the cut-off line and switched back to good old reflector housings.  Because my SUV is tall to begin with and lifted 2&#8243;, I had to angle the beam path down to not blind cars when I pulled up behind them at a red light.  This killed the my down road range, making everything close to me brightly illuminated but the stuff down the road that I needed to see was completely black.  </p>
<p>I dont see why everyone is so fascinated with focusing light in such a way that results in sharp cut-off regions.  I see it with flashlights and headlamps too.  Turn the intensity down and stick with a more natural distribution of the light!</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->

							</footer><!-- .comment-meta -->

			<div class="comment-content">
				<p>No one forced you to lift your suv that resulted in blinding the driver in front of you at stops. You have no one to blame for lowering your new hid lights so you wouldn&#8217;t blind the driver at stops. While well intentioned and being courteous, the lift may be more harm than good. </p>
<p>As you may or may not know,  hid projector headlights are designed with a cutoff plate inside to cut off the upper beam in a low beam projector otherwise it becomes a high beam to blind everyone. You don&#8217;t have to believe me. Do your research on hid projector lighting. Some euro vehicles have this cutoff plate on a solenoid, effectively allowing the low beam to become a high beam when the cutoff plate is moved out of the way.</p>
			</div><!-- .comment-content -->

STEP 4(QUESTION 2): READ ARTICLE HTML FROM FILE AND PRINT TEXT CONTENT.¶

Read in your article's html source from the file you created in question 1 and print it's text (use .get_text())

In [15]:
# The text of the article is in the article element
# read the article html from the pickle file
with open('article.pkl', 'rb') as file:
    article_html1 = pickle.load(file)

# read the article html from the text file
with open('article.txt', 'r', encoding='utf-8') as file:
    article_html2 = file.read()

# Create a BeautifulSoup object
soup1 = BeautifulSoup(article_html1, 'html5lib')
soup2 = BeautifulSoup(article_html2, 'html5lib')

# Find the article element
article_element1 = soup1.find('article')
article_element2 = soup2.find('article')

# Print the first 3000 characters of article text to verify
# that the content is correctly loaded from both files
print("Article text from pickle file:")
print(article_element1.get_text()[:3000])
print()  # Print an empty line
print("Article text from text file:")
print(article_element2.get_text()[:3000])  

Article text from pickle file:

    
        How Laser Headlights Work
        
            
                130 Comments            
            
                by:
                Lewin Day
            
        

        
            March 22, 2021
            
                
                
            
        

        
    

    
        When we think about the onward march of automotive technology, headlights aren’t usually the first thing that come to mind. Engines, fuel efficiency, and the switch to electric power are all more front of mind. However, that doesn’t mean there aren’t thousands of engineers around the world working to improve the state of the art in automotive lighting day in, day out.
Sealed beam headlights gave way to more modern designs once regulations loosened up, while bulbs moved from simple halogens to xenon HIDs and, more recently, LEDs. Now, a new technology is on the scene, with lasers!

Laser Headlights?!
BWM’s prototype laser headlight assemblies undergoing testing.
The first image brought to mind by the phrase “laser headlights” is that of laser beams firing out the front of an automobile. Obviously, coherent beams of monochromatic light would make for poor illumination outside of a very specific spot quite some distance away. Thankfully for our eyes, laser headlights don’t work in this way at all.
Instead, laser headlights consist of one or more solid state laser diodes mounted inside the headlight. These blue lasers are fired at a yellow phosphor, similar to that used in white LEDs. This produces a powerful, vibrant white light that can then be bounced off reflectors and out of the headlights towards the road. Laser headlights built in this way have several benefits. They’re more energy efficient than LEDs that put out the same amount of light, while also being more space efficient, too.
BWM’s futuristic i8 was one of the first vehicles to ship with laser headlight technology.
Laser headlights are still a nascent technology, thus far only appearing in a few BMW, Audi, and other select vehicles. BMW’s technology was developed in partnership with lighting experts OSRAM. In practice, a regular LED low-beam lamp is used, with the laser used to create an incredibly bright and focused spot, used for high-beams. This can provide illumination out to 600 meters ahead of the vehicle, double that of conventional LED high beams. The lights use indium gallium nitride diode lasers that were originally used in projectors, with power levels above 1 watt. One of the challenges in implementing such technology in an automotive environment is the need for it to operate at temperature extremes. While research lasers and laser pointers may primarily be used at typical room temperatures, automotive headlights must be able to withstand everything from 40 degrees below zero up to 50 degrees C. Thankfully, the laser’s high efficiency means it doesn’t have huge heat output of its own to further complicate things. Other engineering challenges involve tailoring th

            March 22, 2021
            
                
                
            
        

        
    

    
        When we think about the onward march of automotive technology, headlights aren’t usually the first thing that come to mind. Engines, fuel efficiency, and the switch to electric power are all more front of mind. However, that doesn’t mean there aren’t thousands of engineers around the world working to improve the state of the art in automotive lighting day in, day out.
Sealed beam headlights gave way to more modern designs once regulations loosened up, while bulbs moved from simple halogens to xenon HIDs and, more recently, LEDs. Now, a new technology is on the scene, with lasers!

Laser Headlights?!
BWM’s prototype laser headlight assemblies undergoing testing.
The first image brought to mind by the phrase “laser headlights” is that of laser beams firing out the front of an automobile. Obviously, coherent beams of monochromatic light would make for poor illumination outside of a very specific spot quite some distance away. Thankfully for our eyes, laser headlights don’t work in this way at all.
Instead, laser headlights consist of one or more solid state laser diodes mounted inside the headlight. These blue lasers are fired at a yellow phosphor, similar to that used in white LEDs. They’re more energy efficient than LEDs that put out the same amount of light, while also being more space efficient, too.
BWM’s futuristic i8 was one of the first vehicles to ship with laser headlight technology.
Laser headlights are still a nascent technology, thus far only appearing in a few BMW, Audi, and other select vehicles. BMW’s technology was developed in partnership with lighting experts OSRAM. In practice, a regular LED low-beam lamp is used, with the laser used to create an incredibly bright and focused spot, used for high-beams. This can provide illumination out to 600 meters ahead of the vehicle, double that of conventional LED high beams. The lights use indium gallium nitride diode lasers that were originally used in projectors, with power levels above 1 watt. One of the challenges in implementing such technology in an automotive environment is the need for it to operate at temperature extremes. While research lasers and laser pointers may primarily be used at typical room temperatures, automotive headlights must be able to withstand everything from 40 degrees below zero up to 50 degrees C. Thankfully, the laser’s high efficiency means it doesn’t have huge heat output of its own to further complicate things. Other engineering challenges involve tailoring th

STEP 5(QUESTION 3): TOKENIZATION OF ARTICLE TEXT.¶

Load the article text into a trained spaCy pipeline, and determine the 5 most frequent tokens (converted to lower case). Print the common tokens with an appropriate label. Additionally, print the tokens their frequencies (with appropriate labels). Make sure to remove things we don't care about (punctuation, stopwords, whitespace).

In [19]:
from collections import Counter
# Load the spaCy model
nlp = spacy.load("en_core_web_sm")


# Process the article text
doc1 = nlp(article_element1.get_text())
doc2 = nlp(article_element2.get_text())

# Get the tokens from the article text,converting to lower case and removing punctuation, stopwords, and whitespace
# for both the pickle file and the text file
tokens1 = [token.text.lower() for token in doc1 if not token.is_punct and not token.is_stop and not token.is_space]
tokens2 = [token.text.lower() for token in doc2 if not token.is_punct and not token.is_stop and not token.is_space]

# Count the frequency of each token
token_freq1 = Counter(tokens1) 
token_freq2 = Counter(tokens2)

# print tokens and their frequencies for the first 5 most common tokens
common_tokens1 = token_freq1.most_common(5)
common_tokens2 = token_freq2.most_common(5)
print("5 Most frequent tokens from pickle file:")
for token, freq in common_tokens1:
    print(f"Token: {token}, Frequency: {freq}")
print()  # Print an empty line
print("5 Most frequent tokens from text file:")
for token, freq in common_tokens2:
    print(f"Token: {token}, Frequency: {freq}")

5 Most frequent tokens from pickle file:
Token: laser, Frequency: 35
Token: headlights, Frequency: 19
Token: headlight, Frequency: 11
Token: technology, Frequency: 10
Token: led, Frequency: 10

5 Most frequent tokens from text file:
Token: laser, Frequency: 35
Token: headlights, Frequency: 19
Token: headlight, Frequency: 11
Token: technology, Frequency: 10
Token: led, Frequency: 10

STEP 6(QUESTION 4): LEMMATIZATION OF ARTICLE TEXT¶

Load the article text into a trained spaCy pipeline, and determine the 5 most frequent lemmas (converted to lower case). Print the common lemmas with an appropriate label. Additionally, print the lemmas with their frequencies (with appropriate labels). Make sure to remove things we don't care about (punctuation, stopwords, whitespace).

In [47]:
from collections import Counter
# Load the spaCy model
nlp = spacy.load("en_core_web_sm")


# Process the article text
doc1 = nlp(article_element1.get_text())
doc2 = nlp(article_element2.get_text())

# get the lemmas from the article text, converting to lower case and removing punctuation, stopwords, and whitespace
# for both the pickle file and the text file
lemmas1 = [token.lemma_.lower() for token in doc1 if not token.is_punct and not token.is_stop and not token.is_space]
lemmas2 = [token.lemma_.lower() for token in doc2 if not token.is_punct and not token.is_stop and not token.is_space]

# Count the frequency of each lemma
lemma_freq1 = Counter(lemmas1) 
lemma_freq2 = Counter(lemmas2)

# print lemmas and their frequencies for the first 5 most common lemmas
common_lemmas1 = lemma_freq1.most_common(5)
common_lemmas2 = lemma_freq2.most_common(5)
print("5 Most frequent lemmas from pickle file:")
for lemma, freq in common_lemmas1:
    print(f"Lemma: {lemma}, Frequency: {freq}")
print()  # Print an empty line
print("5 Most frequent lemmas from text file:")
for lemma, freq in common_lemmas2:
    print(f"Lemma: {lemma}, Frequency: {freq}")

5 Most frequent lemmas from pickle file:
Lemma: laser, Frequency: 40
Lemma: headlight, Frequency: 28
Lemma: beam, Frequency: 12
Lemma: technology, Frequency: 10
Lemma: led, Frequency: 10

5 Most frequent lemmas from text file:
Lemma: laser, Frequency: 40
Lemma: headlight, Frequency: 28
Lemma: beam, Frequency: 12
Lemma: technology, Frequency: 10
Lemma: led, Frequency: 10

STEP 7(QUESTION 5)¶

Define the following methods: * score_sentence_by_token(sentence, interesting_token) that takes a sentence and a list of interesting token and returns the number of times that any of the interesting words appear in the sentence divided by the number of words in the sentence * score_sentence_by_lemma(sentence, interesting_lemmas) that takes a sentence and a list of interesting lemmas and returns the number of times that any of the interesting lemmas appear in the sentence divided by the number of words in the sentence

You may find some of the code from the in class notes useful; feel free to use methods (rewrite them in this cell as well). Test them by showing the score of the first sentence in your article using the frequent tokens and frequent lemmas identified in question 3.

In [66]:
import string

def score_sentence_by_token(sentence, interesting_token):
    """
    Score a sentence based on the presence of interesting tokens.
    
    Args:
        sentence (str): The sentence to score.
        interesting_token (list): A list of interesting tokens.
        
    Returns:
        float: The score of the sentence.
    """
    translator = str.maketrans('', '', string.punctuation)  # Remove punctuation
    cleaned_sentence = sentence.translate(translator)  # Clean the sentence
    cleaned_sentence = cleaned_sentence.lower()  # Convert to lower case
    print(f"Cleaned sentence: {cleaned_sentence}")  # Debugging line to check cleaned sentence
    # Split the cleaned sentence into words
    words = cleaned_sentence.split()
    print(f"Words in sentence: {words}")  # Debugging line to check words
    # Check if there are any words in the sentence
    if len(words) == 0:
        return 0.0
    count = sum(1 for word in words if word.lower() in interesting_token)
    print(f"Count of interesting tokens in sentence: {count}")  # Debugging line to check count
    print(f"Total words in sentence: {len(words)}")  # Debugging line   
    return round(count / len(words),5)

def score_sentence_by_lemma(sentence, interesting_lemmas):
    """
    Score a sentence based on the presence of interesting lemmas.
    
    Args:
        sentence (str): The sentence to score.
        interesting_lemmas (list): A list of interesting lemmas.
        
    Returns:
        float: The score of the sentence.
    """
    translator = str.maketrans('', '', string.punctuation)  # Remove punctuation
    cleaned_sentence = sentence.translate(translator)  # Clean the sentence
    cleaned_sentence = cleaned_sentence.lower()  # Convert to lower case
    print(f"Cleaned sentence: {cleaned_sentence}")  # Debugging line to check cleaned sentence
    # Split the cleaned sentence into words
    words = cleaned_sentence.split()
    print(f"Words in sentence: {words}")  # Debugging line to check words
    # Check if there are any words in the sentence
    if len(words) == 0:
        return 0.0
    count = sum(1 for word in words if word.lower() in interesting_lemmas)
    print(f"Count of interesting lemmas in sentence: {count}")  # Debugging line to check count
    print(f"Total words in sentence: {len(words)}")  # Debugging line
    return round(count / len(words), 5)

# Example usage
initial_paragraph= article_element1.find('p').get_text()  # Get the first paragraph of the article
first_sentence = initial_paragraph.split('.')[0]  # Get the first sentence
print("First sentence:", first_sentence)  # Print the first sentence
sentence = first_sentence.strip()  # Remove leading/trailing whitespace
# Extract just the tokens (without frequencies) from the common_tokens1 list
interesting_tokens = [token for token, freq in common_tokens1]  # Use the first 5 common tokens from the pickle file
print("Interesting tokens:", interesting_tokens)  # Print the interesting tokens
interesting_lemmas = [lemma for lemma, freq in common_lemmas1]  # Use the first 5 common lemmas from the pickle file
print("Interesting lemmas:", interesting_lemmas)  # Print the interesting lemmas
print(score_sentence_by_token(sentence, interesting_tokens))  # Score by token
print(score_sentence_by_lemma(sentence, interesting_lemmas))  # Score by lemma string

STEP 8(QUESTION 6)¶

Make a list containing the scores (using tokens) of every sentence in the article, and plot a histogram with appropriate titles and axis labels of the scores. From your histogram, what seems to be the most common range of scores (put the answer in a comment after your code)?

#use the same methods to score every sentence in the article
def score_article_by_token(article_text, interesting_tokens):
    """
    Score an article based on the presence of interesting tokens in each sentence.
    
    Args:
        article_text (str): The text of the article.
        interesting_tokens (list): A list of interesting tokens.
        
    Returns:
        list: A list of scores for each sentence in the article.
    """
    doc = nlp(article_text)
    scores = []
    for sent in doc.sents:
        score = score_sentence_by_token(sent.text, interesting_tokens)
        
        scores.append(score)
    return scores


scores = score_article_by_token(article_element1.get_text(), interesting_tokens)


# plot on histogram the scores for each sentence
plt.figure(figsize=(10, 6))
plt.hist(scores, bins=20, color='blue', alpha=0.7)
plt.title("Distribution of Sentence Scores by Token")
plt.xlabel('Score')
plt.ylabel('Frequency')
plt.grid(axis='y', alpha=0.75)
plt.ylim(0,4)
plt.xlim(0, 1)
plt.show()

# Answer: From the histogram, we can see that the most common range of scores 0 and 0.1.

STEP 9(QUESTION 7)¶

Make a list containing the scores (using lemmas) of every sentence in the article, and plot a histogram with appropriate titles and axis labels of the scores. From your histogram, what seems to be the most common range of scores (put the answer in a comment after your code)?

In [69]:
# perform the same operation for the lemmas
def score_article_by_lemma(article_text, interesting_lemmas):
    """
    Score an article based on the presence of interesting lemmas in each sentence.
    
    Args:
        article_text (str): The text of the article.
        interesting_lemmas (list): A list of interesting lemmas.
        
    Returns:
        list: A list of scores for each sentence in the article.
    """
    doc = nlp(article_text)
    scores = []
    for sent in doc.sents:
        score = score_sentence_by_lemma(sent.text, interesting_lemmas)
        scores.append(score)
    return scores
scores_lemmas = score_article_by_lemma(article_element1.get_text(), interesting_lemmas)
# plot on histogram the scores for each sentence
plt.figure(figsize=(10, 6))
plt.hist(scores_lemmas, bins=20, color='green', alpha=0.7)    
plt.title("Distribution of Sentence Scores by Lemma")
plt.xlabel('Score')
plt.ylabel('Frequency')
plt.grid(axis='y', alpha=0.75)
plt.ylim(0,4)
plt.xlim(0, 1)
plt.show()

STEP 10(QUESTION 8)¶

Which tokens and lexems would be ommitted from the lists generated in questions 3 and 4 if we only wanted to consider nouns as interesting words? How might we change the code to only consider nouns? Put your answer in this Markdown cell (you can edit it by double clicking it).

Answer:¶

Based on my analysis in question 3 and 4, no word in the most common tokens and lemmas would be ommitted since they are all nouns. How ever if we want to exclude non-nouns, we can filter tokens and lemmas based on their part-of-speech tags. An example of a code we can utilize is:

common_tokens_nouns = [token for token, freq in common_tokens1 if nlp.vocab[token].pos_ == 'NOUN'] common_lemmas_nouns = [lemma for lemma, freq in common_lemmas1 if nlp.vocab[lemma].pos_ == 'NOUN']
